<p><b>Elizabeth Ann Warren</b> (née <b>Herring</b>; born June 22, 1949)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> is an American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic" title="Academic">academic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">politician</a>. She is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>, and is the <a href="/wiki/Seniority_in_the_United_States_Senate" title="Seniority in the United States Senate">senior</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>. Warren was formerly a professor of law, and taught at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_School_of_Law" title="University of Texas School of Law">University of Texas School of Law</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Law_School" title="University of Pennsylvania Law School">University of Pennsylvania Law School</a>, and most recently at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a>. A prominent scholar specializing in <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_in_the_United_States" title="Bankruptcy in the United States">bankruptcy law</a>, Warren was among the most cited in the field of <a href="/wiki/Commercial_law" title="Commercial law">commercial law</a> before starting her political career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren is an active <a href="/wiki/Consumer_protection" title="Consumer protection">consumer protection</a> advocate whose scholarship led to the conception and establishment of the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a>. She has written a number of academic and popular works, and is a frequent subject of media interviews regarding the American economy and personal finance. Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_financial_crisis" title="2008 financial crisis">2008 financial crisis</a>, Warren served as chair of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congressional_Oversight_Panel" title="Congressional Oversight Panel">Congressional Oversight Panel</a> created to oversee the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP). She later served as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assistant_to_the_President#Organization" title="Assistant to the President">Assistant to the President</a> and Special Advisor to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> for the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau under <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>. During the late 2000s, she was recognized by publications such as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Law_Journal" title="National Law Journal">National Law Journal</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Time_100" title="Time 100"><i>Time</i> 100</a> as an increasingly influential public policy figure.</p>
<p>In September 2011, Warren announced her candidacy for the U.S. Senate, challenging <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> incumbent <a href="/wiki/Scott_Brown_(politician)" title="Scott Brown (politician)">Scott Brown</a>. She won the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts,_2012" title="United States Senate election in Massachusetts, 2012">general election</a> on November 6, 2012, becoming the first female Senator from Massachusetts. She was assigned to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senate_Special_Committee_on_Aging" title="Senate Special Committee on Aging">Senate Special Committee on Aging</a>; the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Banking,_Housing,_and_Urban_Affairs" title="United States Senate Committee on Banking, Housing, and Urban Affairs">Banking, Housing, and Urban Affairs Committee</a>; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Health,_Education,_Labor,_and_Pensions" title="United States Senate Committee on Health, Education, Labor, and Pensions">Health, Education, Labor, and Pensions Committee</a>.</p>
<p>Warren is a leading figure in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and is popular among <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States" title="Progressivism in the United States">American progressives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Although she repeatedly stated that she was not running for president,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nightmare_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-nightmare-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coulddefinitely_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-coulddefinitely-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notgoingtorun_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-notgoingtorun-8">[8]</a></sup> Warren was frequently mentioned by political pundits as a strong potential candidate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 presidential elections</a>. Warren remained neutral during the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 2016">2016 Democratic presidential primaries</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> endorsing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presumptive_nominee" title="Presumptive nominee">presumptive nominee</a> <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> only after all fifty states had voted. On July 7, <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> reported that Warren was on a five-person shortlist to be Clinton's <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">vice-presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Running_mate" title="Running mate">running mate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endorsesclinton_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-endorsesclinton-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, Clinton eventually chose <a href="/wiki/Tim_Kaine" title="Tim Kaine">Tim Kaine</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life.2C_education.2C_and_family">Early life, education, and family</span></h2>
<p>Warren was born on June 22, 1949,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Unwinding_page_345_to_350_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Unwinding_page_345_to_350-15">[15]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City" title="Oklahoma City">Oklahoma City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> parents Pauline (<i>née</i> Reed) and Donald Jones Herring; Warren has described her family as teetering "on the ragged edge of the middle class" and "kind of hanging on at the edges by our fingernails."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bierman2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bierman2-17">[17]</a></sup> She was their fourth child, with three older brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanity_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-18">[18]</a></sup> When she was 12, her father, a janitor at <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_Ward" title="Montgomery Ward">Montgomery Ward</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bierman2_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bierman2-17">[17]</a></sup> had a heart attack—which led to many medical bills, as well as a pay cut because he could not do his previous work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-19">[19]</a></sup> Eventually, this led to the loss of their car from failure to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_loan" title="Car loan">loan</a> payments. To help the family finances, her mother found work in the catalog order department at <a href="/wiki/Sears" title="Sears">Sears</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-20">[20]</a></sup> When she was 13, Warren started waiting tables at her aunt's restaurant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanity_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren became a star member of the debate team at <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Classen_High_School" title="Northwest Classen High School">Northwest Classen High School</a> and won the title of "Oklahoma's top high school debater" while competing with debate teams from high schools throughout the state. She also won a debate scholarship to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a> at the age of 16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-19">[19]</a></sup> Initially aspiring to be a teacher, she left GWU after two years to marry her high school boyfriend, Jim Warren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanity_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bberg_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-bberg-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren moved to <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a> with her husband, who was a <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> engineer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-22">[22]</a></sup> There she enrolled in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Houston" title="University of Houston">University of Houston</a>, graduating in 1970 with a bachelor of science degree in speech pathology and audiology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bostonian-2009_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bostonian-2009-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> For a year, she taught children with disabilities in a public school, based on an "emergency certificate," as she had not taken the education courses required for a regular teaching certificate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren and her husband moved for his work to New Jersey, where, after becoming pregnant, she decided to remain at home to care for their child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conversations_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-30">[30]</a></sup> After their daughter turned two, Warren enrolled at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rutgers_School_of_Law%E2%80%93Newark" title="Rutgers School of Law–Newark">Rutgers School of Law–Newark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conversations_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-30">[30]</a></sup> She worked as a summer associate at <a href="/wiki/Cadwalader,_Wickersham_%26_Taft" title="Cadwalader, Wickersham &amp; Taft">Cadwalader, Wickersham &amp; Taft</a>. Shortly before her graduation in 1976, Warren became pregnant with their second child. After receiving her <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">J.D.</a> and passing the <a href="/wiki/Bar_examination" title="Bar examination">bar examination</a>, she began to work as a lawyer from home, writing wills and doing real estate closings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bberg_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-bberg-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conversations_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>After having two children, Amelia and Alexander, she and Jim Warren divorced in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tenthings_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-tenthings-31">[31]</a></sup> She also has grandchildren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1980, Elizabeth married <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Mann_(legal_historian)" title="Bruce Mann (legal historian)">Bruce Mann</a>, a law professor, but retained the surname Warren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tenthings_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-tenthings-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_affiliation">Political affiliation</span></h3>
<p>Warren voted as a Republican for many years, saying, "I was a Republican because I thought that those were the people who best supported markets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-22">[22]</a></sup> According to Warren, she began to vote Democratic in 1995 because she no longer believed that to be true, but she states that she has voted for both parties because she believed that neither party should dominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="581" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg/170px-Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg/255px-Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg/340px-Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_CFPB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren discussing the work of the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Community_Bankers_of_America" title="Independent Community Bankers of America">ICBA</a> conference in 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the late 1970s, 1980s, and 1990s, Warren taught law at several universities throughout the country while researching issues related to <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy" title="Bankruptcy">bankruptcy</a> and middle-class <a href="/wiki/Personal_finance" title="Personal finance">personal finance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conversations_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-30">[30]</a></sup> She became involved with public work in bankruptcy regulation and consumer protection in the mid-1990s.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic">Academic</span></h3>
<p>Warren started her academic career as a lecturer at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rutgers_School_of_Law%E2%80%93Newark" title="Rutgers School of Law–Newark">Rutgers School of Law–Newark</a> (1977–78). She moved to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Houston_Law_Center" title="University of Houston Law Center">University of Houston Law Center</a> (1978–83), where she became Associate Dean for Academic Affairs in 1980, and obtained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenure" title="Tenure">tenure</a> in 1981. She taught at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_School_of_Law" title="University of Texas School of Law">University of Texas School of Law</a> as visiting associate professor in 1981, and returned as a full professor two years later (staying 1983–87). In addition, she was a visiting professor at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> (1985) and research associate at the Population Research Center of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> (1983–87).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WarrenCV2008_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-WarrenCV2008-35">[35]</a></sup> Early in her career, Warren became a proponent of on-the-ground research based on studying how people actually respond to laws in the real world. Her work analyzing court records, and interviewing judges, lawyers, and debtors, established her as a rising star in the field of bankruptcy law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neyfakh_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neyfakh-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren joined the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania_Law_School" title="University of Pennsylvania Law School">University of Pennsylvania Law School</a> as a full professor in 1987 and obtained an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endowed_chair" title="Endowed chair">endowed chair</a> in 1990 (becoming William A Schnader Professor of Commercial Law). She taught for a year at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a> in 1992 as Robert Braucher Visiting Professor of Commercial Law. In 1995, Warren left Penn to become Leo Gottlieb Professor of Law at Harvard Law School.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WarrenCV2008_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-WarrenCV2008-35">[35]</a></sup> As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Elizabeth_Warren&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, she was the only tenured law professor at Harvard who had attended law school at an American public university.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neyfakh_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neyfakh-36">[36]</a></sup> Warren was a highly influential law professor. Although she published in many fields, her expertise was in bankruptcy and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_law" title="Commercial law">commercial law</a>. In that field only <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Scott" title="Robert E. Scott">Bob Scott</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Law_School" title="Columbia University Law School">Columbia</a> and Alan Schwartz of <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale</a> had citation rates higher than that of Warren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advisory_roles">Advisory roles</span></h3>
<p>In 1995, Warren was asked to advise the National Bankruptcy Review Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> She helped to draft the commission's report and worked for several years to oppose legislation intended to severely restrict the right of consumers to file for bankruptcy. Warren and others opposing the legislation were not successful; in 2005 Congress passed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_Abuse_Prevention_and_Consumer_Protection_Act_of_2005" title="Bankruptcy Abuse Prevention and Consumer Protection Act of 2005">Bankruptcy Abuse Prevention and Consumer Protection Act of 2005</a>, which curtailed the ability of consumers to file for bankruptcy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>From November 2006 to November 2010, Warren was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">FDIC</a> Advisory Committee on Economic Inclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> She is a member of the National Bankruptcy Conference, an independent organization that advises the U.S. Congress on bankruptcy law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> She is a former Vice President of the <a href="/wiki/American_Law_Institute" title="American Law Institute">American Law Institute</a> and a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren's scholarship and public advocacy was the impetus behind the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_life">Public life</span></h3>
<p>Warren has a high public profile; she has appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary films</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Maxed_Out" title="Maxed Out">Maxed Out</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Capitalism:_A_Love_Story" title="Capitalism: A Love Story">Capitalism: A Love Story</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rose_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rose-46">[46]</a></sup> She has appeared numerous times on television programs, including <i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Phil_(TV_series)" title="Dr. Phil (TV series)">Dr. Phil</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Show" title="The Daily Show">The Daily Show</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and has been interviewed frequently on cable news networks and radio programs.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="TARP_oversight">TARP oversight</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="560" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg/260px-Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg/390px-Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg/520px-Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nomination_of_Richard_Cordray.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren stands next to President Barack Obama as he announces the nomination of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cordray" title="Richard Cordray">Richard Cordray</a> as the first director of the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">CFPB</a>, July 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 14, 2008, Warren was appointed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senate_Majority_Leader" title="U.S. Senate Majority Leader">U.S. Senate Majority Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Reid" title="Harry Reid">Harry Reid</a> to chair the five-member <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congressional_Oversight_Panel" title="Congressional Oversight Panel">Congressional Oversight Panel</a> created to oversee the implementation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_Economic_Stabilization_Act" title="Emergency Economic Stabilization Act">Emergency Economic Stabilization Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The Panel released monthly oversight reports that evaluate the government bailout and related programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kantor_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kantor-49">[49]</a></sup> During Warren's tenure, these reports covered foreclosure mitigation, consumer and small business lending, commercial real estate, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIG" title="AIG">AIG</a>, bank stress tests, the impact of the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP) on the financial markets, government guarantees, the automotive industry, and other topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[a]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</span></h3>
<p>Warren was an early advocate for the creation of a new <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a> (CFPB). The bureau was established by the <a href="/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" title="Dodd–Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd–Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act</a> signed into law by President Obama in July 2010. In September 2010, President Obama named Warren <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assistant_to_the_President" title="Assistant to the President">Assistant to the President</a> and Special Advisor to the Secretary of the Treasury on the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau to set up the new agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">liberal</a> groups and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consumer_advocacy" title="Consumer advocacy">consumer advocacy</a> groups pushed for Obama to formally nominate Warren as the agency's director, Warren was strongly opposed by financial institutions and by <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> members of Congress who believed Warren would be an overly zealous regulator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanity_Fair-Andrews-2011-07_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanity_Fair-Andrews-2011-07-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Wyatt-2011-07-04_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Wyatt-2011-07-04-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Rosenthal-2011-12-08_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Rosenthal-2011-12-08-54">[53]</a></sup> Reportedly convinced that Warren could not win Senate confirmation as the bureau's first director,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTNov8_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTNov8-55">[54]</a></sup> Obama turned to former <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Attorney_General" title="Ohio Attorney General">Ohio Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cordray" title="Richard Cordray">Richard Cordray</a> and in January 2012, over the objections of Republican senators, appointed Cordray to the post in a <a href="/wiki/Recess_appointment" title="Recess appointment">recess appointment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Senate">U.S. Senate</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_election">2012 election</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="949" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg/280px-2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg/420px-2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg/560px-2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2012_US_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts_results_by_municipality.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
2012 Senate election results by municipality</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts,_2012" title="United States Senate election in Massachusetts, 2012">United States Senate election in Massachusetts, 2012</a></div>
<p>On September 14, 2011, Warren declared her intention to run for the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> nomination for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts,_2012" title="United States Senate election in Massachusetts, 2012">2012 election in Massachusetts</a> for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate">U.S. Senate</a>. The seat had been won by Republican <a href="/wiki/Scott_Brown_(politician)" title="Scott Brown (politician)">Scott Brown</a> in a 2010 special election after the death of <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> A week later, a video of Warren speaking in <a href="/wiki/Andover,_Massachusetts" title="Andover, Massachusetts">Andover</a> became a <a href="/wiki/Viral_video" title="Viral video">viral video</a> on the Internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> In it, Warren replies to the charge that asking the rich to pay more taxes is "class warfare," pointing out that no one grew rich in the U.S. without depending on infrastructure paid for by the rest of society, stating:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is nobody in this country who got rich on his own. Nobody. ... You moved your goods to market on the roads the rest of us paid for; you hired workers the rest of us paid to educate; you were safe in your factory because of police forces and fire forces that the rest of us paid for. You didn't have to worry that marauding bands would come and seize everything at your factory, and hire someone to protect against this, because of the work the rest of us did. Now look, you built a factory and it turned into something terrific, or a great idea. God bless. Keep a big hunk of it. But part of the underlying social contract is, you take a hunk of that and pay forward for the next kid who comes along.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> later <a href="/wiki/You_didn%27t_build_that" title="You didn't build that">echoed her sentiments</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama_presidential_campaign,_2012" title="Barack Obama presidential campaign, 2012">2012 election campaign speech</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2012, the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Herald" title="Boston Herald">Boston Herald</a></i> sparked a campaign controversy when it reported that from 1986 to 1995 Warren had listed herself as a minority in the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Law_Schools" title="Association of American Law Schools">Association of American Law Schools</a> (AALS) directories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard_trips_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard_trips-64">[63]</a></sup> Harvard Law School had publicized her minority status in response to criticisms about a lack of faculty diversity, but Warren said that she was unaware of this until she read about it in a newspaper during the 2012 election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard_trips_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard_trips-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmichael_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carmichael-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-66">[65]</a></sup> Scott Brown, her Republican opponent in the Senate race, speculated that she had fabricated Native American heritage to gain advantage in the job market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wash_Post_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wash_Post-69">[68]</a></sup> Former colleagues and supervisors at universities where she had worked stated that Warren's ancestry played no role in her hiring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmichael_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carmichael-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wash_Post_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wash_Post-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> Warren responded to the allegations, saying that she had self-identified as a minority in the directories in order to meet others with similar tribal roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Her brothers defended her, stating that they "grew up listening to our mother and grandmother and other relatives talk about our family's Cherokee and Delaware heritage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SallyJacobs_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-SallyJacobs-72">[71]</a></sup> In her 2014 autobiography, Warren described the allegations as untrue and hurtful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_England_Historic_Genealogical_Society" title="New England Historic Genealogical Society">New England Historic Genealogical Society</a> found a family newsletter that alluded to a marriage license application that listed Elizabeth Warren’s great-great-great grandmother as a Cherokee, but could not find the primary document and found no proof of her descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wash_Post_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wash_Post-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No_proof_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-No_proof-75">[74]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_Historical_Society" title="Oklahoma Historical Society">Oklahoma Historical Society</a> said that finding a definitive answer about Native American heritage can be difficult because of intermarriage and deliberate avoidance of registration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="1884" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg/170px-Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg/255px-Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg/340px-Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_Nov_2_2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren at a campaign event, November 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Warren ran unopposed for the Democratic nomination and won it on June 2, 2012, at the state Democratic convention with a record 95.77% of the votes of delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rizzuto_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rizzuto-77">[76]</a></sup> She was endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Massachusetts" title="Governor of Massachusetts">Governor of Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deval_Patrick" title="Deval Patrick">Deval Patrick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Warren and her opponent Scott Brown agreed to engage in four televised debates, including one with a consortium of media outlets in Springfield and one on <a href="/wiki/WBZ-TV" title="WBZ-TV">WBZ-TV</a> in Boston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> She encountered significant opposition from business interests. In August 2012, Rob Engstrom, political director for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Chamber_of_Commerce" title="U.S. Chamber of Commerce">U.S. Chamber of Commerce</a>, claimed that "no other candidate in 2012 represents a greater threat to free enterprise than Professor Warren."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> She nonetheless raised $39 million for her campaign, the most of any Senate candidate in 2012, and showed, according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, "that it was possible to run against the big banks without Wall Street money and still win."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTNov8_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTNov8-55">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren received a primetime speaking slot at the <a href="/wiki/2012_Democratic_National_Convention" title="2012 Democratic National Convention">2012 Democratic National Convention</a>, immediately before <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, on the evening of September 5, 2012. Warren positioned herself as a champion of a beleaguered middle class that "has been chipped, squeezed, and hammered." According to Warren, "People feel like the system is rigged against them. And here's the painful part: They're right. The system is rigged." Warren said Wall Street CEOs "wrecked our economy and destroyed millions of jobs" and that they "still strut around congress, no shame, demanding favors, and acting like we should thank them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenure">Tenure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="920" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg/280px-Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg/420px-Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg/560px-Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sen._Mo_Cowan_Swearing_In.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren attending the swearing in of Senator <a href="/wiki/Mo_Cowan" title="Mo Cowan">Mo Cowan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Senate_Chamber" title="Old Senate Chamber">Old Senate Chamber</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 6, 2012, Warren defeated incumbent Scott Brown with a total of 53.7% of the votes. She is the first woman ever elected to the U.S. Senate from Massachusetts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_members_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Current members of the United States Congress">a sitting U.S. Senate</a> that had 20 female senators in office, the largest female U.S. Senate delegation in history, following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_elections,_2012_and_2013" title="United States Senate elections, 2012 and 2013">the November 2012 elections</a>. In December 2012, Warren was assigned a seat on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Banking,_Housing,_and_Urban_Affairs" title="United States Senate Committee on Banking, Housing, and Urban Affairs">Senate Banking Committee</a>, the committee that oversees the implementation of Dodd–Frank and other regulation of the banking industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Warren was sworn in by Vice President <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a> on January 3, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> Upon <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a>'s resignation to become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_State" title="U.S. Secretary of State">U.S. Secretary of State</a>, Warren became the state's senior senator after having served for less than a month, making her the <a href="/wiki/Seniority_in_the_United_States_Senate" title="Seniority in the United States Senate">most junior senior senator</a> in the 113th Congress.</p>
<p>At Warren's first Banking Committee hearing on February 14, 2013, she pressed several banking regulators to answer when they had last taken a Wall Street bank to trial and stated, "I'm really concerned that 'too big to fail' has become 'too big for trial'." Videos of Warren's questioning became popular on the Internet, amassing more than 1 million views in a matter of days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> At a Banking Committee hearing in March, Warren asked <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">Treasury Department</a> officials why criminal charges were not brought against <a href="/wiki/HSBC" title="HSBC">HSBC</a> for its <a href="/wiki/HSBC#Money_laundering" title="HSBC">money laundering</a> practices. With her questions being continually dodged and her visibly upset, Warren then compared <a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a> to drug possession, saying: "If you're caught with an ounce of cocaine, the chances are good you're going to go to jail... But evidently, if you launder nearly a billion dollars for drug cartels and violate our international sanctions, your company pays a fine and you go home and sleep in your own bed at night."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2013, Warren sent letters to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a>, questioning their decisions that settling rather than going to court would be more fruitful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Later that month, Warren introduced her first bill, the Bank on Student Loans Fairness Act, which would allow students to take out government education loans at the same rate that banks such as <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan_Chase" title="J.P. Morgan Chase">J.P. Morgan Chase</a> pay to borrow from the federal government. Suggesting that students should get "the same great deal that banks get", Warren proposed that new student borrowers be able to take out a federally subsidized loan at 0.75%, the rate paid by banks, compared with the current 3.4% student loan rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Endorsing her bill days after its introduction, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_(politician)" title="Independent (politician)">Independent</a> Senator from <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a> stated: "The only thing wrong with this bill is that [she] thought of it and I didn't" on <i><a href="/wiki/Thom_Hartmann" title="Thom Hartmann">The Thom Hartmann Program</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 2014 election cycle, Warren was a top Democratic fundraiser, supporting candidates in Ohio, Minnesota, Oregon, Washington, West Virginia, Michigan, and Kentucky. In the aftermath of the election, Warren was appointed by Majority Leader Harry Reid (the same man who made her chair of the Congressional Oversight Panel) to become the first-ever Strategic Advisor of the Democratic Policy and Communications Committee, a position that was created just for her. The move was widely seen as an effort by Reid to lean his party more to the left following major Democratic losses in the recent election; it also boosted further speculation about a possible presidential run by Warren in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saying, "despite the progress we've made since 2008, the biggest banks continue to threaten our economy," in July 2015 Senator Warren, along with John McCain (R-AZ), Maria Cantwell (D-WA), and Angus King (I-ME) re-introduced the 21st Century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glass-Steagall_Act" title="Glass-Steagall Act">Glass-Steagall Act</a>, a modern version of the Banking Act of 1933. The legislation is intended to reduce the risk for the American taxpayer in the financial system and decrease the likelihood of future financial crises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elizabeth_Warren_U.S._Senator_for_Massachusetts_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elizabeth_Warren_U.S._Senator_for_Massachusetts-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a September 20, 2016 hearing, Warren called for the CEO of <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo" title="Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stumpf" title="John Stumpf">John Stumpf</a>, to resign, adding that he should be "criminally investigated" over Wells Fargo's opening of two million checking and credit-card accounts without the consent of their customers under his tenure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnewswellsfargobossurgedtoresign_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnewswellsfargobossurgedtoresign-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessinsider_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-businessinsider-98">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Committee_assignments">Committee assignments</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Banking,_Housing,_and_Urban_Affairs" title="United States Senate Committee on Banking, Housing, and Urban Affairs">Committee on Banking, Housing, and Urban Affairs</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Banking_Subcommittee_on_Economic_Policy" title="United States Senate Banking Subcommittee on Economic Policy">Subcommittee on Economic Policy</a> (Ranking Member)</li>

<ul>

</ul>
</li>

<ul>

</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_positions">Political positions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_positions_of_Elizabeth_Warren" title="Political positions of Elizabeth Warren">Political positions of Elizabeth Warren</a></div>
<p>According to the UK magazine <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i>, Warren is among the "top 20 US progressives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newstatesman_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-newstatesman-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 16, 2016, Warren announced that she plans to introduce a bill to address future president Donald Trump's perceived conflicts of interest related to his business empire. Under her proposed bill Donald Trump could face impeachment if he fails to declare conflicts of interest between his presidential role and his business interests. Warren states, “The only way for President-elect Trump to truly eliminate conflicts-of-interest is to divest his financial interests and place them in a blind trust. This has been the standard for previous presidents, and our bill makes clear the continuing expectation that President-elect Trump do the same.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Independent_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Independent-100">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 9, 2017, a bill introduced by Warren, the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Conflicts_of_Interest_Act" title="Presidential Conflicts of Interest Act">Presidential Conflicts of Interest Act</a>, was first read in the Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s65-sentate_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-s65-sentate-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2016_speculation">2016 speculation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="851" data-file-width="768" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_Manchester_NH_October_2016.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren campaigning for <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Hillary Clinton presidential campaign, 2016">Hillary Clinton</a> (seated) in <a href="/wiki/Manchester,_New_Hampshire" title="Manchester, New Hampshire">Manchester, New Hampshire</a> in October 2016.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the run-up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2016_U.S._presidential_election" title="2016 U.S. presidential election">2016 U.S. presidential election</a>, Warren's name was put forward by liberal Democrats as a possible presidential candidate. However, Warren repeatedly stated that she was not running for President in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nightmare_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-nightmare-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coulddefinitely_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-coulddefinitely-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notgoingtorun_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-notgoingtorun-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> In October 2013, she joined with the other fifteen Senate Democratic women in signing a letter that encouraged <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> to run.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> There was much speculation about Warren being added to the Democratic ticket as a vice-presidential candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> On June 9, 2016, after the <a href="/wiki/California_Democratic_primary,_2016" title="California Democratic primary, 2016">California Democratic primary</a>, Warren formally endorsed Hillary Clinton for president. In response to questions when she endorsed Clinton, Warren said that she believed she was ready to be vice president, but she was not being vetted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endorsesclinton_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-endorsesclinton-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren has taken an active role in the 2016 presidential elections, and has publicly feuded with Republican presumptive nominee <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>, in speeches and on Twitter, pointing to his business practices and describing him as dishonest, uncaring of people and "a loser".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pocahontas_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-pocahontas-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> In return, Trump has mocked her for her description of her Native American heritage, calling her "the Indian", "goofy" and "<a href="/wiki/Pocahontas" title="Pocahontas">Pocahontas</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pocahontas_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-pocahontas-110">[109]</a></sup> Warren also criticized Trump for his stance on the <a href="/wiki/Trump_University" title="Trump University">Trump University</a> case, calling him a "loud, nasty, thin-skinned fraud who has never risked anything for anyone and serves nobody but himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2018_election">2018 election</span></h3>
<p>On January 6, 2017 in an <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">e-mail</a> to supporters, Warren announced that she would be running for a second term as US Senator from Massachusetts. She wrote in the e-mail, “The people of Massachusetts didn’t send me to Washington to roll over and play dead while Donald Trump and his team of billionaires, bigots, and Wall Street bankers crush the working people of our Commonwealth and this country,” and “This is no time to quit.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_awards">Honors and awards</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2103" data-file-width="1602" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/140px-Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/210px-Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/280px-Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Warren_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Warren at the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Time_100" title="Time 100"><i>Time</i> 100</a> Gala</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2009, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i> named her the Bostonian of the Year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bostonian-2009_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bostonian-2009-24">[24]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Bar_Association_of_Massachusetts" title="Women's Bar Association of Massachusetts">Women's Bar Association of Massachusetts</a> honored her with the <a href="/wiki/Lelia_J._Robinson" title="Lelia J. Robinson">Lelia J. Robinson</a> Award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> She was named one of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Magazine" title="Time Magazine">Time Magazine</a>'</i>s <a href="/wiki/Time_100" title="Time 100">100 Most Influential People in the World</a> in 2009, 2010 and 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time100_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time100-117">[116]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Law_Journal" title="National Law Journal">National Law Journal</a></i> repeatedly has named Warren as one of the Fifty Most Influential Women Attorneys in America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> and in 2010 it honored her as one of the 40 most influential attorneys of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> In 2011, Warren was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_Hall_of_Fame" title="Oklahoma Hall of Fame">Oklahoma Hall of Fame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> In January 2012, Warren was named one of the "top 20 US progressives" by the British <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newstatesman_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-newstatesman-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, Warren became the first professor in Harvard's history to win the law school's The Sacks–Freund Teaching Award for a second time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> In 2011, she delivered the commencement address at the Rutgers School of Law–Newark, where she was awarded an honorary Doctor of Laws degree and was conferred membership in the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Coif" title="Order of the Coif">Order of the Coif</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Books_and_other_works">Books and other works</span></h2>
<p>Warren and her daughter Amelia Tyagi wrote <i>The Two-Income Trap: Why Middle-Class Mothers and Fathers Are Going Broke</i>. Warren and Tyagi point out that a fully employed worker today earns less inflation-adjusted income than a fully employed worker did 30 years ago. Although families spend less today on clothing, appliances, and other consumption, the costs of core expenses such as mortgages, health care, transportation, and child care have increased dramatically. The result is that even with two income earners, families are no longer able to save and have incurred greater and greater debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an article in <i>The New York Times</i>, <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Madrick" title="Jeff Madrick">Jeff Madrick</a> said of Warren's book:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The authors find that it is not the free-spending young or the incapacitated elderly who are declaring bankruptcy so much as families with children ... their main thesis is undeniable. Typical families often cannot afford the high-quality education, health care, and neighborhoods required to be middle class today. More clearly than anyone else, I think, Ms. Warren and Ms. Tyagi have shown how little attention the nation and our government have paid to the way Americans really live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 2005, Warren and David Himmelstein published a study on bankruptcy and medical bills,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> which found that half of all families filing for bankruptcy did so in the aftermath of a serious medical problem. They say that three-quarters of such families had medical insurance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warren_wapo_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-warren_wapo-126">[125]</a></sup> This study was widely cited in policy debates, although some have challenged the study's methods and offered alternative interpretations of the data, suggesting that only seventeen percent of bankruptcies are directly attributable to medical expenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langer_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-langer-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Warren's book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Fighting_Chance" title="A Fighting Chance">A Fighting Chance</a></i> was published by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Holt_and_Company" title="Henry Holt and Company">Metropolitan Books</a> in April 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> According to a review published in <i>The Boston Globe</i>, "[t]he book's title refers to a time she says is now gone, when even families of modest means who worked hard and played by the rules had at a fair shot at the American dream."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
